RATING: 4
This game just didn't work for me. I think it could work if it were recast as a purely competitive or purely cooperative game. It didn't help that the game was incredibly easy to win with the settings we played on, but I think that the system is fundamentally flawed.

Semi-cooperative games can work when you need to balance your personal needs against the needs of the group as a whole. In Marvel, doing what's best for you will usually be good for the group as well, and hurting the other players a little bit incidentally won't really matter if it ever comes up in the first place. Semi-cooperative works much better in Archipelago.

If it wants to be cooperative, it needs to be MUCH harder. Regardless of whether it wants to be cooperative or competitive, it would really benefit from more interesting card interactions. Cooperatively it's just easy and boring. Basically the opposite of Ghost Stories. Competitively it's not interesting, and the theme isn't well integrated. Basically a less interesting and more bland Ascension.

#5 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2016)
#2 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2015)

After falling in love with Legendary: Encounters, we decided to check out the original Marvel version. So happy we did as we like it even more! We own everything Legendary and keep an ear out for any and all new expansions on the horizon. I've printed colorful dividers! The amount of combinations of heroes/villains/schemes is immense. Sometimes, you might get hosed by a particular tough combo, but it's still fun for me. I don't mind some randomness in games when they are cooperative in nature. We never play Legendary competitively. We may count up points afterwards, just so we know for bragging rights, but during the game- it's all about cooperation and handling the oncoming hoard of enemies as best we can. This game is marvelous!! (See what I did there.)

RATING: N/A
An overall fun game if you enjoy the superhero genre, otherwise it will be hit or miss.  If you play this as a pure co-op the game is somewhat of a joke unless you stack things heavily against you and have the Dark City expansion.  As a competitive game it is really just a take the best card available that you can purchase, lacking much in strategy.

Longer Version:
Competitive - I don't like the game as a competitive game.  It doesn't make sense to me thematically and just feels weird.  You also are really just taking whatever is the best card in front of you.  Very luck dependent on when things happen to appear.
Co-op - That said, the game wasn't meant to be a pure co-op and you can tell.  You need the Dark City expansion to make this even a bit fun.  The base game is just a joke for difficulty.  Even with the expansion you need to be familiar with the heroes + masterminds + schemes to give yourself a challenge.  You can also make it so it is nearly impossible to win with some setups.  There is also random card drawing that can change a setup into impossible to win, or at the same time turn it into a joke.  For the most part the game is just about getting past late game.  If you can survive to the point where your decks have gotten off the ground you have usually won.

RATING: 9
At first glance, this appears to be a simplistic deckbuilder which brings nothing new to the genre and can be played on auto-pilot. If you're a fan of superhero comic books, get this game. It doesn't make you feel like a superhero, but then again nothing does. What it does, however, is make you have fun while using the Marvel heroes we all learned to love. The art is absolutely fantastic, with the images on cards drawn by the Marvel artists. Certainly a game to show off to your non-gaming friends to wow them into the hobby.

RATING: 5
1 play, probably with a bad (random) mix of cards: Mr. Sinister (who likes to capture Bystanders) and Black Widow (who can just kill villains/minions with Bystanders).

The game is sort of a mix of Thunderstone and Ascension, with a comic theme. Villains march into town in random order, have some effects when they appear and when you fight them. Here, they continue marching out of town (escaping), triggering a scenario-specific result. Similarly, heroes available for purchase march into display randomly. Each turn you draw a villain card (either a villain, or a card triggering some effect). Then use your hand: may use every icon and all text on all cards (i.e., you don't get to/have to choose between which parts of a card to use). At the end, discard all your cards and draw a new hand. But with no cheap-cost things to do (unlike Ascension, which has 1-cost cards and 2-cost monsters) you can end up just sitting around hoping for a good draw!

I was so bored -- very happy when the game ended! I might try this again, if someone really wants to play it; but it doesn't do a lot for me!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I gave this a 9 because I like the Marvel theme, but there's lots missing to this game that the expansions (sometimes successfully, sometimes not) fill in. The starting game is not too complex for a Deck Builder. Sure, there are tons of cards on the table but it doesn't take too much to understand what happens each turn and what you have to do.

The real fun of this game you only get about half the time which is what my friends and I refer to as "popping off" which is when you get enough cards that your deck is actually working as intended and firing on all cylinders. I would say the two biggest flaws with this game are that this does not happen often enough and that some characters are way better than others and it's not always the fan favorite characters or most powerful characters.

Also something the expansions fix is that the character cards all have the same art.

Still the game is quite fun. I love the co-operative element which makes it feel like you are teaming together to take down the Red Skull or the like.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is a mix between Ascension and Thunderstone that is played more cooperatively.  Like those games and many deck-builders before it I can already see myself losing interest with it after a number of plays without them releasing (and me buying) expansions for it.

I was super-excited for this game, it was the one game I wanted most for Christmas and with the Amazon gift cards I received for Christmas it was the first thing I bought.  I used to be a huge comic book junkie so obviously I knew I would love the theme (and I do).  From what I already knew about the game I even knew that its biggest failing would be its limited life span which is the biggest weakness (for me) of the deck-building game genre altogether.

The game has several other weaknesses though that I didn't (and couldn't) know until after receiving my copy and playing it.  The most glaring of which is the competitive element of the game and how it is integrated.  At first I thought it wouldn't be such a big deal; you play the game cooperatively and either win or lose as a team but at the end of the game each player receives points based on how much they contributed to the win.  That's not such a big deal, it's an added mechanic that comes "out-of-box" that I thought could just be completely ignored without affecting the game.  And it can be mostly ignored (which is what I would suggest because competing for victory points can hurt a cooperative experience as players compete for which cards give them the best chance to rack up victory points instead of discussing whose deck can best take advantage of those cards "for the team").  Where it still affects the game though is in the design of some of the cards.  A big example of which is one of the Hawkeye cards which gives you the option of forcing all other players to draw or discard a card.  I see no situation where forcing the other players to discard cards would be beneficial to them, and therefore the team.  Granted I haven't played with all the cards yet so there very well may be some positive synergy between discarding cards and other card effects.  You may also call this nitpicking since I could always just choose the "draw" option but the fact that the card is designed to allow you to hurt other players seems like a poor design choice to me and like I'm paying for a feature of the card when I recruit it into my deck that I won't ever use.  It also probably means other cards are designed with a similar "gotcha" mechanic on them.  All-in-all I just don't think "semi-cooperative" games really work.

A couple other of my complaints are small.  I'm annoyed that the game does not include randomizer cards or labeled dividers.  The games I've played so far have also been extremely easy with players defeating the Mastermind 4 times and coming nowhere near losing before getting through more than a quarter of the villain deck each time.  There are more difficult setups and easy ways to increase the difficulty printed in the rulebook so I withhold judgement on the difficulty until after adjusting it and getting more experience with the game.

Pass that, this is a solid enough game.  As I said above I love the theme and it doesn't feel completely pasted on.  Sure, theme is always going to be a little abstracted with a deck-builder but the abilities and stats printed on the cards match the character, scheme, etc. and how it pertains to the Marvel universe pretty well most of the time.  

It is also very well-designed with a lot of elements working well together.  We haven't found anything broken or too confusing yet.  This also points toward the rulebook being well-written as another boon for the game.  The packaging is good with room for expansions (although the box seems a bit bigger height-wise than it needs to be) and the art is beautiful.

All-in-all I had an enjoyable time playing the game.  It's not without its faults, the biggest one being the design of the competitive elements in this cooperative game.  But I can move past that and once I get the difficulty honed in to a more tense and enjoyable experience I can see myself getting a number of plays out of this right up until the inevitable expansion releases that should extend this game's lifespan even more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
My 2nd deck-building game experience, I really love the concept so I imagine it is hard for me to go wrong with a deck builder. Comparisons to the DC game are hard to avoid since thematically so close, I do like the DC better but this one is really fun, too.
Where it falls a bit short is the semi-coop idea. Semi-coop doesn't work for me (at least not here). To work together is fine but when in the end the points count again it easy to get screwed over, e.g. you are the nice guy to take less points or even wounds to make the game as a group, someone else cashes in instead and wins in the end. Can very easily happen, plus, the "together-against-the-game" feeling that makes coops so interesting never happens when you still eye your own points. 
But I had fun, the game is very enjoyable and I would play again. As in owning I would rather go for the DC but that is mostly personal taste I reckon.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It's not bad. Like the theming, though it's a bit pasted on. 

The one major problem that I ran into was that since it's a co-op, there is little incentive to "screw" other players over (intentionally or unintentionally) in order to improve your personal deck. All decisions naturally felt like they had to be most beneficial to the team which made decisions too obvious. In some ways the game felt like it played itself.

The other issue was that there weren't any serious consequences to deal with. Villains escaping, even getting wounded by a mastermind didn't really feel like it mattered that much. Might have to up the difficulty next time I play. 

All that being said my friends and I always like to make things competitive and amassing victory points, regardless of how meaningless and petty, was plenty of motivation for us to go at it. It played less like a co-op game and more like the board game equivalent of a multiplayer game of the new Mario platformers with everyone scrambling to beat everyone else even at the expense of others. 

When you play with a lighthearted mentality going into it, it's a lot of fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I can not recommend this game enough. The base game can be really light on variety by itself with only 15 heroes, but with over a dozen expansions it really opens up endless possibilities with over 100 heroes.

Things I love about this game...
-Highly modular nature making no two set ups feel the same.
-Great art on the cards
-Great use of "mechanics as metaphor" such as the Spiderman and wolverine cards all doing low damage but power draw your deck simulating a flurry of attacks where cyclops and hulk have heavy hitting cards with drawbacks or how Thor has to "charge" his hammer to get damage by having recruit thresholds.
-Cooperative or competitive play styles are both welcome as you can opt to work together or fight tooth and nail to snag all the victory points you can.

As I stated earlier, the base game is a little light on variety, so I would highly recommend a couple of expansions. I would say the must haves are...
-dark city
-civil war
-x-men
-paint the town red
-guardians of the galaxy

RATING: 10
My favorite game of all time! I've played over 1000 advanced solo games...this is not a typo! Obviously I wouldn't have played it so much if it wasn't amazingly fun. Entertaining schemes, endless combinations (so the game never gets boring), and most importantly, Marvel heroes! Game Designer Devin Low did a fantastic job of throwing together all the best parts of deck-building games with unsurpassed thematic tie-ins. And you can even change the level of difficulty to make the game as challenging as you'd like. But as fun and addictive as this game is, you will not be able to stop buying the expansions. Each one adds more flavor and more challenges. But don't take my word for it, try it for yourself. You won't be disappointed.

RATING: 7.5
One of the more popular deckbuilding systems around today -- and for good reason! While the game is not as thematic as some players would like (you're more like SHIELD giving orders to the heroes than the heroes themselves), being invested in Marvel (or super heroes/comics) at some level will really add to your enjoyment of the game. The semi-cooperative aspect of the game is take it or leave it (it's "leave it" for me), but the mechanics work well overall. 

Ultimately, does Legendary: Marvel revolutionize the deckbuilding genre? No; it's rather derivative, in fact. However, what it does, it does well! If you're into deckbuilders or Marvel, give this one a shot.

RATING: 9.5
Something of a cross between Ascension and Thunderstone, but with Marvel Superhero theme. The theme really works here. The villans and the plots are a lot like a crazy comic series with your favorite heroes. 

The only drawback to the base is that it suffers from what most deckbuilders do when they first come out - a serious need for MORE cards. Yep, I'm ready for an expansion already. Oh, that and the setup/teardown time.

**UPDATE Dec 2014
One of my favorite games. I had to do some custom work with dividers and had to toss the insert that came with it (really a poor insert), but I can (barely) fit ALL the current expansions (through Guardians of the Galaxy) sleeved in the main box. The game still has setup/teardown pain, but the story of the game is still a blast to me.

**UPDATE Sept 2017
With the release of DXP (the digital re-theme) of the game, I realized how much I like this game and bumped it to a 9.5 (it is only not a 10 because of setup and teardown)

RATING: 8
There are definitely flaws with this game (like the fact that they don't actually use the city track at all, the game scales very differently for 2-5 players, and the setup and tear down take a while).  BUT, I still have a lot of fun playing it.  I enjoy Legendary more than Dominion, but it obviously doesn't have the same depth and replayability with all the Dominion expansions.  I really like that you can play it cooperatively or competitively because it is like two different games in one.  The Marvel theme isn't as rich in the gameplay as it could have been, but I still think it's the best Marvel game out there.

Update: The Dark City expansion fixes many of the flaws in the base game.  Most of my plays are 2 player co-op with my wife, so I was very pleased to see the increased difficulty.

RATING: 6
This game is the gold standard for Deck Builders. Hero Realms is better at what it does, but Legendary is just so great in how much it can do. Mix and match your masterminds, villains, henchmen, scenario, and yes, your team... The heroes you pick interact ENORMOUSLY with your opposition, a solid team for one can be utterly disadvantaged elsewhere. Crank the difficulty up or down, plot endlessly with friends about set up possibilities, cackle and memorialize epic overkill wins, laugh at sudden massive defeats, and narrative to the way a game plays out to have your own stories. Play two players, play four players, play solo... do a full co-op, risk it all playing cutthroat to see who an nab high score. It's  replay isn't infinite, it took many, many games, but I'm a bit sick of it now. One thing I will say? Consider the Villain set heavily. Instead of the base set might be a bit weird, but multiple stages of design advancement really show in Villians and the core cardpool for players and challenges is much more fun.

RATING: 9
Popped my board game cherry, my first real board game purchase and this game is going to eat all my money. I'm a HUGE Marvel fan and this is the game I've waited for my whole life. The mechanics are not only sound but make sense for the theme. Spider man doesn't do a lot of damage but he's cheap and slings his other cards into action, and can give you a good idea of what's coming. Skrull can put a hurting on you (Super Skrull FTW) but their especially deadly when the utilize their shapeshifting ability. The ability to mix schemes with Masterminds and villains makes every game different. The game scales incredibly well, I've played with 4 people, I've played by myself. It's a great game for older kids, my 12 year old loves it, it's a great game for Dungeons and Dragons players. If you want a game that's high on replay, heavy on theme and elegant on rules, give this game a shot. If you're a fan of Marvel, you've wasted too much time reading this, buy this game.

RATING: 9.5
How do I even begin with this game? It has everything that I look for in board games (more notably card based games):replay value, strategy, and theme. Nothing can top the replay value of this game: 8 different Schemes, different Villian groups, Heroes, Masterminds, there are abundant combinations. Strategy is minor but it's there, just enough for a card game. The theme is great, as all the art was fabricated exclusively for the game. I have heard plenty of complaints on the bordering system of the cards, but I actually like this idea. I went in knowing I was going to love this game, and I do. I think it will be my go to card game now, in loo of Dominion (no hate on Dominion, however). This game is teachable as well, and not too hard to understand. Upper Deck really accomplished something with this game. Expansions are DEFINITELY a future investment. Great game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a strong deck building game that will please fans of the genre, and offer some new elements to people that may not have enjoyed it in the past.

Legendary borrows from a few different titles, and seems to have the hit the right mix.  It is very similar to the Star Trek deck builder, with a smattering of Thunderstone, but it delivers a better experience than either in less time.

Each player gets to see an interaction with the villains every turn (for the most part)
***I've seen a few people comment that they don't like this is a co-op.  It isn't REALLY a co-op game, and you can mess with the other players.  Basically everyone loses or an individual wins.  You are definitely acquiring your own victory points, and you can wound other players intentionally if you want to.  I'd call this semi-coop at best.

My one gripe is that I wish the Hero deck had a bit more diversity during the game.  I realize they are intentionally allowing for combos, but it would be cool if they had gone for stockpiles of teams vs a single card or guy.  (ie, less iterations of spider man and more spidey characters like black cat, etc.)

12/4/2013 - Update

The veneer is completely off of this one for me.  At the end of the day... Legendary is just not all that fun.  The mechanic is very simple regardless of which scenario you're running or who you're up against.  You draw a bunch of cards with pictures of dudes, and have turns ranging from fair to epic.

People call this game thematic, but at the end of the day it really isn't all that much.  The variety of dudes and objectives is cool, but it doesn't translate to variety of actual gameplay or fun factor.

It feels cumbersome and non-engaging, and that is a damn shame.  I don't know what could make it better... it feels like it should awesome and it is just fair.

Dropping the rating to a 6 barring some kind of epiphany.  If you're looking for a great Super Hero card game, check out Sentinels of the Multiverse which is a much more thematic romp.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[thing=129437][/thing] is a deck builder that brings some innovation to the genre, but not all that much. It owes many things to [thing=69789][/thing] and, of course [thing=36218][/thing]. It also has a lot of similarities to  [thing=125678][/thing], but the development of these occurred in parallel, so no foul play here.

[b]Which are the good points?[/b]
- [b]It is an easy game to learn and play[/b] (specially if you are used to any of the other games I mentioned earlier). In the first game we already used a random set up, and have no problem at all with this (well, expect a discussion to see if a color was really different from other);
- The [b]symbols[/b] in the cards, though they are tiny, are, for me,easy to see and to use;
- The [b]texts in the cards[/b] are clear and concise - in none of the cards was necessary to look into the manual to understand how they worked;
- [b] The art[/b]. It is good and with enough variation.

[b]Which are the neutral points?[/b]
[b]- Theme.[/b] It makes more sense than the one in [thing=125678][/thing], where the villains cards help your hero to attack other heroes, in order for you to win. In this game, the villains stay out of the deck (though it would be better if they didn't, in order to clog the deck, since, thematic, the villain would require constant attention to be held at bay). It is nice that the heroes can "help" each other in order to produce some combos;
[b]- The row of cards.[/b] I'm definitely not a fan of the row of cards for purchase. That thing that you buy some OK card, even with a lot of recruiting value, just to see an awesome card (that you could even take yourself, if it was, you know, there before). However, [thing=129437][/thing] saves itself a little for two things: 1) the heroes cards aren't worth victory points, which is different from what happens in [thing=125678][/thing], where not only the great card do some great effect, it is also worth a higher amount of points; and, 2) the row for the villains doesn't get "updated" everytime a villain is defeated, so this lessens the overall impact of the row (and, if you happen to have a tremendous attack, and no major villain to beat in the row, you can, and should, beat the Mastermind). Also, the villains appeared to be well balanced in their values and effects;
[b]- Cooperation.[/b] The heroes are, in thesis, all fighting the Mastermind, his scheme and his minions. So, there is cooperation? Well, there is, but a tinny little small bit. In the two plays I had, only a card from Emma Frost allowed the other players to draw new cards, if they had a X-Men card already in hand; and another card, that the player could choose another player to heal wounds; finally, in after defeating one of the "faces" of the Mastermind, it was possible to choose if the other players would draw one card or discard one card (it was choosen to discard). Of course, usually all the players will try to prevent the losing condition in the Scheme card to happen, and this we did. But there is nothing (except honor! And not being a jackass) to stop a player from giving up the fight, to make everyone losing a closer reality. It is basically as cooperative as [thing=18602][/thing], in which everyone work together to build the kings castle and citadel, and if someone uses a building of mine, I gain something - hey, cooperation!

[b]Which are the bad points?[/b]
[b]- The set up.[/b] It is sort of long. Not worst than the one in [thing=36218][/thing], but sure way higher than the one in [thing=125678][/thing];
[b]- The rich gets richer effect[/b] :shake: Seriously, what is the matter with these hero deck builders games with this point? The case here is less bad than the one I found in [thing=125678][/thing], but still is here. The defeated villains are the cards that are worth victory points (and, also, bystanders rescued). But they don't enter into your deck. So, it requires no ponderation whatsoever as to when you should stop the bulk of recruiting (preparation phase, several movies have it) and start beating villains. Maybe I was spoiled by the fine ways of [thing=36218][/thing] in this matter. In [thing=36218][/thing], with limited buys in your turn, and, mostly, because of the victory points cards serve as a break in every deck, you have always to consider [u]timming[/u] in every play. For me, for as long as the deck builders work around getting victory point cards, the cards that give you this, should remain as a natural break in the deck acceleration. In [thing=129437][/thing], once you achieve a value to beat any villain in the row, you will do it, without a second thought. Got an even higher value? Sweet! Beat some more! So there is no worries about "Damn, if I buy another Estate, my deck will be a swamp", nor "Great, now I got 8 money. What now? Do I buy a Province? This soon? It will stay in my deck the whole game...". Anyway, [thing=129437][/thing] deals with this better than his DC brother, if by nothing else, by nothing using the villains as great cards for any deck, and also by creating a sense of urgency that requires the heroes to start the punching soon.
But I still see this as a bad thing. If one player purchases one, two major great cards (whose others copies might never show up) it can get way ahead, with little chance for the other players to catch up. In [thing=36218][/thing] the victory cards would, usually, make this strong cards don't appear as often, and, even when they do come up, they could end up in a victory cards clogged hand.
I know this decision makes [thing=129437][/thing] to be easier to be introduced to new and/or less experienced players, since the timming issue in [thing=36218][/thing]  is something more complicated to be had and explained (and, also, the timming will change accordingly along with the sets of cards and what the others are doing), but in the long term this decision will be, for me, a bad one.

[b]Final thoughts:[/b]

[thing=129437][/thing] is a good game, with clear and functional mechanics. It is somwhat easy to explain and even if player with some background in [i]deck building[/i] games having a little advantage at first, even first timers will be able to make a nice heroic showing. The plays are usually quick, lasting around 40 to 50 minutes, and after them I stayed with a "want more" feel, not only because of the theme (which is dear to me), but also to see the interactions between other Masterminds, Schemes and Heroes.

The weak points in [thing=129437][/thing] are, in a lesser degree and amount, the same ones I notice in [thing=125678][/thing]: lack of thematic cohesion (for some reason I still keep wanting this in deack builders, but, so far, only [thing=53953][/thing] pleased me in this matter); the row of cards for purchase and fight; and the damned rich gets richer. However, even these points weren't enough to prevent me from enjoying the game - I still have a nice time playing. So I'm ready to play some more!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The jury's still out on whether or not I'll like this as a multiplayer game (semi-cooperative is still cooperative, dang it), but I like the mechanisms of the game so far.  For one, they mesh with the theme better than the DC Comics deck-builder does, if only because players are all buying out of the same pool of heroes, instead of taking on the role of a hero and then taking on other heroes' superpowers.  It makes more sense for players to be working together as a team to take down the villains, even if they're all still trying to take down the most in one game.  In a way, players are basically managers of S.H.I.E.L.D. branches, each vying to get the best quarterly report from the main office.  

I'm interested in playing the game again, and playing with other opponents, just to see how it plays out.  As a solitaire game, it's OK, and as a deck-builder, I haven't delved into it deeply enough to know what sorts of tactics exist among all the mastermind, henchmen, villain, and hero combinations.  But it's intriguing so far.

UPDATE: I've played it with three players, and it's actually not a bad cooperative game.  The tension of the villain deck adds something to the game, and as I mentioned above, the game fits the superhero theme better than the DC Comics deck-builder did.  I also appreciate that it's semi-cooperative, so there's still a winner, but even if you lose, you can take some satisfaction from having defeated evil along the way.

One thing I find frustrating about the game so far is that it's difficult to thin your deck, which is a necessary tactic for most deck-builders.  It's too easy to wind up with a lot of dreck in your deck, and I wish there were more feasible ways to alleviate that issue.

2 plays: 1 player
1 play: 3 players

RATING: 7
This is easily the better of the two superhero deckbuilders. It's sort of a competitive co-op, with players trying to assemble the best version of their own custom Avengers team to take on a Villain who is in turn trying to pull off some crazy scheme. Because of this, the game usually tells a great story of hostages, rescues, bank robberies and all sorts of cosmic emergencies. Every single game truly is unique.

The downside is that the level of customization creates an immense set-up/take down time commitment. Cards have to be sorted, shuffled, then resorted when you are done. It is truly a pain...but well worth it for the experience, I think.

Card quality is wanting, the fragility of the cards demands sleeves and at least in the base set, the game leans toward being too easy to defeat the villian. Thankfully, that was addressed in all of the expansion materials that have been released, so now it takes real cooperation to defeat the villain and real guile to beat the other players to that S.H.I.E.L.D promotion.

If you love comic books or comic book movies, you should grab this, ASAP.

RATING: 8
I can't quite put my finger on why I like Legendary so much. I know part of it is theme, but it's not like the theme is heavily integrated into the mechanics. Mechanically, it's pretty similar to Ascension in a lot of ways, but I hate Ascension. I guess the differences have a substantial effect on my enjoyment. In particular, feeling like you're struggling against a threat as well as opponents and the way there are separate tracks for things to buy and things to attack (as opposed to Ascension' shared area for both) make the game more enjoyable. 

My two complaints are that the theme often feels fairly pasted on and that it's typically pretty easy (at least in the base set), but it's a lot of fun for a deckbuilder.

RATING: 8
My favorite "pure" deck-building game. A little bit of that love may be owed to the theme and the bright and beautiful artwork that accompanies it. But you can't make me like a game just by slapping a bunch of spandex and energy blasts on it. No, the underlying game is a solid one, basically taking the formula of Ascension and improving upon it.

And those improvements really do make a difference here. Separating the enemies from the deck-building cards. Giving you an objective (Mastermind) to complete and special obstacles (Scheme Card) to overcome. Encouraging you to tailor the available card selection to create different combos or increase the challenge.

That's really what I find the most fun about Marvel Legendary: it's extreme flexibility. You can create all kinds of different scenarios by how you choose to mix and match the game's set-up.

The base game is pretty good on it's own and gives you plenty to work with but with even just one "big box" expansion (for my money, it's Dark City), you can really blow the lid off this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

RATING: 6.5
Surprisingly good. Very innovative considering the game is 1) and Upper Deck production and 2) a deck building game - based on established IP no less!

This actually captures "semi-cooperative" fairly well which is something I think many other games attempt and fail miserably at. The game certainly seems to have a small bit of balance issues (though I suppose a superhero game should be a fair bit swingy). I can't decide if I like that the art is repetitive for gameplay or if I'd rather have individual pieces of art for each card for a more immersive experience. 

Bottom line: Fun little game that plays quickly.

RATING: 8
Marvel Legendary may or may not be a better design than Dominion, but it is a lot more fun.  Which means that it actually [i]is[/i] better than Dominion in the only way that counts.

I don't see any serious gamers jettisoning Dominion and rushing out to purchase Legendary, but there are 1001 families and comic fans that would rather have this box under the holiday tree than the Big D.

And if there's one thing that I've never been accused of, it's being serious.

[i]Lesson learned:[/i]

Having to set up the game from scratch every time is atrocious, and reason enough to avoid playing.  So I don't do that.  Instead, I always have a pre-constructed scenario ready to go (including all decks) for three players, which is our most common player count.  This means that we can get up and running in just a few minutes.

To keep things fresh, every three to four sessions I'll reset the game by rotating in new heroes and villains, a new scheme, or an entirely new expansion. Which there are millions of, at this point, and I've happily purchased every one.

RATING: 5
Might have been a 6 a year ago or a 7 two years ago, but this is such a generic deckbuilding game today.  All it really has going for it is theme.  Game play is really simple and dull.  You basically just lay out your cards and add up your recruitment and combat value.  If the cards have combos they pretty much trigger by themselves. My only gripe is pre-game setup. I don't mean every game, I mean the first one. Open the box and sort every card, in order to be able to take the cards you need and shuffle them back up. It didn't really take long, but it felt like a chore that would never end.

RATING: 8
Probably my second favorite deckbuilding game, right after Ascension (and then, I'd say it only loses out due to higher set-up time).

Very thematic and I love the semi-coop aspect (once you get used to the different characters and know how to optimize your deck, you start being aggressive towards other players right from the get-go :P ).
